				<div id="bodyContent" class="vector-body" aria-labelledby="firstHeading" data-mw-ve-target-container>
					<div class="vector-body-before-content">
							<div class="mw-indicators">
		</div>

						<div id="siteSub" class="noprint">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
					</div>
					<div id="contentSub"><div id="mw-content-subtitle"></div></div>
					
					
					<div id="mw-content-text" class="mw-body-content"><div class="mw-content-ltr mw-parser-output" lang="en" dir="ltr"><div class="shortdescription nomobile noexcerpt noprint searchaux" style="display:none">Polynesian language spoken in Vanuatu</div>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1066479718">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:auto}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-header,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-subheader,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-above,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-title,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-image,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data,body.skin-minerva .mw-parser-output .infobox-below{text-align:center}</style><table class="infobox vevent"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above above" style="font-size:125%; color: black; background-color: pink;">Futuna-Aniwa</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;">Region</th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;">Native speakers</div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;">1,500&#160;(2001)<sup id="cite_ref-e18_1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e18-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><div style="display: inline-block; line-height: 1.2em; padding: .1em 0;"><span class="wrap"><a href="/wiki/Language_family" title="Language family">Language family</a></span></div></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><div style="text-align:left;"><a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian</a>
<ul style="line-height:100%; margin-left:1.35em;padding-left:0"><li>
<a href="/wiki/Malayo-Polynesian_languages" title="Malayo-Polynesian languages">Malayo-Polynesian</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Oceanic_languages" title="Oceanic languages">Oceanic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Polynesian_languages" title="Polynesian languages">Polynesian</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><a href="/wiki/Futunic_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Futunic languages">Futunic</a><ul style="line-height:100%;margin-left:0.45em;padding-left:0;"><li><b>Futuna-Aniwa</b></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></li></ul></div></td></tr><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-header" style="color: black; background-color: pink;">Language codes</th></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><span class="nowrap"><a href="/wiki/ISO_639-3" title="ISO 639-3">ISO 639-3</a></span></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><a href="https://iso639-3.sil.org/code/fut" class="extiw" title="iso639-3:fut">fut</a></code></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label" style="white-space:nowrap;padding-right:0.65em;"><i><a href="/wiki/Glottolog" title="Glottolog">Glottolog</a></i></th><td class="infobox-data" style="line-height:1.3em;"><code><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://glottolog.org/resource/languoid/id/futu1245">futu1245</a></code></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p><b>Futuna-Aniwa</b> is a language spoken in the Tafea Province of <a href="/wiki/Vanuatu" title="Vanuatu">Vanuatu</a> on the <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_outlier" title="Polynesian outlier">outlier</a> islands of <a href="/wiki/Futuna_Island,_Vanuatu" title="Futuna Island, Vanuatu">Futuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Aniwa_Island" title="Aniwa Island">Aniwa</a>. The language has approximately 1,500 speakers.<sup id="cite_ref-e18_1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-e18-1">&#91;1&#93;</a></sup> It is a <a href="/wiki/Polynesian_language" class="mw-redirect" title="Polynesian language">Polynesian language</a>, part of the Austronesian language family.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">&#91;2&#93;</a></sup>
</p><p>It is occasionally called <b>West Futunan</b> to distinguish it from <a href="/wiki/East_Futunan" class="mw-redirect" title="East Futunan">East Futunan</a> spoken on the islands of <a href="/wiki/Futuna_(Wallis_and_Futuna)" title="Futuna (Wallis and Futuna)">Futuna</a> and <a href="/wiki/Alofi_Island" title="Alofi Island">Alofi</a> in <a href="/wiki/Wallis_and_Futuna" title="Wallis and Futuna">Wallis and Futuna</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The phonology of Futuna-Aniwa is mostly similar to the phonology of Polynesian (Capell, 1984). However, there are some elements that are not consistent in Futuna-Aniwa with Polynesian (Capell, 1984). For example, in Futuna-Aniwa both <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_lateral_approximants" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar lateral approximants">l</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dental,_alveolar_and_postalveolar_trills" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental, alveolar and postalveolar trills">r</a> are present, although Polynesian languages normally have one or the other (Capell, 1984). Similarly, another distinction can be made between <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">/s/</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_sibilant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar sibilant">/ʃ/</a> in Futuna, and <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_alveolar_fricative" title="Voiceless alveolar fricative">/s/</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar affricate">/tʃ/</a> in Aniwa, which is also not normal in Polynesian (Capell, 1984).
</p>

<ul><li>/p/ - Devoiced bilabial unaspirated plosive, initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/t/ - Denti-alveolar devoiced unaspirated plosive, initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/k/ - Devoiced velar unaspirated plosive, front and back varieties depending on neighbouring vowel but not differing phonemically; initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/m/ - Voiced bilabial nasal, initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/n/ - Voiced denti-alveolar nasal; initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/ŋ/ - Voiced velar nasal, initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/f/ - Voiceless bilabial fricative in both languages, but often occurring in Aniwa for Futuna v; initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/l/ - Voiced alveolar lateral, fricative, initial, medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/r/ - Voiced lingual flapped alveolar consonant, sometimes practically fricative, without phonemic difference (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/s/ - Voiceless alveolar sibilant, initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/ʃ/ - Voiceless palato-alveolar fricative with some lip-rounding. Futuna sound, to which /<a href="/wiki/Voiceless_palato-alveolar_affricate" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiceless palato-alveolar affricate">tʃ</a>/ corresponds in Aniwa; initial and medial (Capell, 1984).</li></ul>

<td>

<td><a href="/wiki/Mid_back_rounded_vowel" title="Mid back rounded vowel">o</a>
</td></tr>

<th><a href="/wiki/Low_vowel" class="mw-redirect" title="Low vowel">Low</a>

</td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ul><li>/i/ - High front unrounded vowel, of moderate tenseness, but relaxed (without lowering) if unstressed (Capell, 1984). Occurs in all positions (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/e/ - Open mid front unrounded vowel, phonetically [ɛ] in all positions (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/a/ - Low open vowel in all positions (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/o/ - Open mid back rounded vowel, phonetically [ɔ], occurs in all positions (Capell, 1984).</li>
<li>/u/ - High back rounded vowel, corresponding to /i/ as regards tenseness; all positions (Capell, 1984). Before and after vowels /i/ and /u/ become semivowels (Capell, 1984).</li></ul>
<p>Looking at the information provided above, it is important to note out the variations of Aniwa compared to Futuna (Capell, 1984). The most important and recognizable variations from a multiple number of other variations is extreme palatalisation of dental consonants before front high and mid vowels, which also in some cases involves n- (Capell, 1984). An example of this is te/ti in Futuna is normally represented as ce/ci in Aniwa (Capell, 1984). Compared to Futuna, in Aniwa the velar plosive is backed more regularly, therefore k, q, and y is heard (Capell, 1984). This is shown most of the time, but there is a little freedom in variation (Capell, 1984). /k/ is retained before front vowels, word-initially, and before diphthongal clusters,  /k/ becomes /q/ before /a/, and /k/ becomes /y/ before /o/ (Capell, 1984). The rule itself first shows that there is consciousness of the word and not just the utterance and secondly, the exceptions may show native ideas of boundaries and junctures (Capell, 1984). These are the main differences between the two dialects, however, it is noted by Capell that the data collected is not accurate or fully reliable from that particular village (Capell, 1984).
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Morphology">Morphology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Morphology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronoun_and_person_markers">Pronoun and person markers</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Pronoun and person markers"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Pronouns in Futuna-Aniwa distinguish for four numbers (singular, dual, trial and plural) and for first (inclusive and exclusive), second and third persons (Dougherty, 1983). The distinction of trial and plural in a Polynesian language is an unusual feature of Futuna-Aniwa (Capell, 1984).
</p><p>There are primarily five different sets of pronominal forms in Futuna-Aniwa: personal, possessive, interrogative, emphatic, and demonstrative. In some circumstances, pronominal clitics will accompany these pronominal forms. (Dougherty, 1983)
</p><p>Pronouns are not inflected for gender, but can be marked for oblique case (marked by <i>i</i>) and, optionally, subjective case (marked by <i>e</i>). (Dougherty, 1983)
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronoun_construction">Pronoun construction</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Pronoun construction"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Notably, pronouns in Futuna-Aniwa can all be easily can be divided into specific morphological components. For example, the second person nonsingular dual pronoun <i>akorua</i> (see below) is formed by combining the personal article prefix <i>a-, </i>the nonsingular, second person, pronominal focus infix <i>–ko-</i> and the dual suffix <i>–(r)ua</i>. (Dougherty, 1983)
</p><p>The only exception to this are interrogative pronouns, whose morphological construction is more complex and variable.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Personal_pronouns">Personal pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Personal pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Personal Pronouns are not obligatory in Futuna-Aniwa and may be omitted where context allows, as evident in example 1.

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Possessive_pronouns">Possessive pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Possessive pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Pronouns in Futuna-Aniwa can be used to indicate inalienable possession, exclusively for singular persons. These appear immediately before an inalienable head noun. (Dougherty, 1983)

<p>There is also an equivalent “paradigm of personal pronoun suffixes” (Dougherty, 1983, p.&#160;33) that occur in possessive constructions, suffixing onto nouns to indicate possession. Note that example (3) below can also be expressed using the above equivalent inalienable possessive pronouns.
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interrogative_pronouns">Interrogative pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Interrogative pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>There are 14 different interrogative pronoun forms in Futuna-Aniwa. Minimally, Futuna-Aniwa distinguishes between singular and nonsingular in all interrogative pronoun constructions. (Dougherty, 1983) Nonsingular interrogative pronouns appear in dual, trial and plural positions. (Capell, 1984)
</p>

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Emphatic_pronouns">Emphatic pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Emphatic pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Emphatic pronouns in Futuna-Aniwa are used to indicate that the designated referent in a clause is the only referent being referred to. (Dougherty, 1983)

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Demonstrative_pronouns">Demonstrative pronouns</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Demonstrative pronouns"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Demonstrative pronouns in Futuna-Aniwa distinguish three positions: position near speaker, position near addressee and distant position. They are in essence a grouping of the base demonstrative form and the Futuna-Aniwa article system (Dougherty, 1983). This is further explained in the <i>Demonstratives</i> section below.
</p>
<table class="wikitable">

<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Pronominal_clitics">Pronominal clitics</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Pronominal clitics"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Within certain constructions in Futuna-Aniwa pronominal clitics occur alongside pronouns. Pronominal clitics only occur with singular persons and serve the role of cross referencing the subject of a verb. The pronominal clitic is typically suffixed onto tense or aspect markers, negatives in preverbal position or the infinitive (Dougherty, 1983).
</p><p>Pronominal clitics primarily occur within transitive constructions in Futuna-Aniwa, although not exclusively (Dougherty, 1983). This is evident through comparing example (8) (a transitive construction) with example (9) (an intransitive construction) below. The pronominal clitic is not present in (9), but is suffixed onto the preverbal negative particle in (8).
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">(8)</div><div>(Dougherty, 1983, p.&#160;37)</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">(avau)</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="first person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">1SG</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">se-n</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="negation/negative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NEG</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="particle" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PART</abbr>-<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="pronoun" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PRO</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="clitic" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">CLI</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">tahtaroa</p><p style="margin: 0px;">wash</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ma</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="negation/negative" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">NEG</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="particle" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PART</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">sa</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="article" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ART</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">kapiti</p><p style="margin: 0px;">cabbage</p></div><p style="display: none;">(avau) se-n tahtaroa ma sa kapiti</p><p style="display: none;">1SG NEG.PART-PRO.CLI wash NEG.PART ART cabbage</p><p style="clear: left;">‘I didn’t wash the cabbage.’</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>Note: In (8), the first personal singular was omitted from the original example. It has been included for clarity.
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">(9)</div><div>(Dougherty, 1983, p.&#160;41)</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">avau</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="first person, singular" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">1SG</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ka</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="future tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">FUT</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">TNS</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">muri</p><p style="margin: 0px;">follow</p></div><p style="display: none;">avau ka muri</p><p style="display: none;">1SG FUT.TNS follow</p><p style="clear: left;">'I'll follow.'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Demonstratives">Demonstratives</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Demonstratives"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Demonstrative" title="Demonstrative">Demonstratives</a> in Futuna-Aniwa distinguish by location relative to the speaker, with three categories for relative distance: close to the speaker, close to the addressee, and far away from both. In natural speech, the distinction between what is close to the listener and what is far away can sometimes be lost, and the respective demonstrative for each can be used somewhat interchangeably in conversational settings. Most constructions in Futuna-Aniwa use a set of three demonstratives: <i>nei,</i> indicating position near the speaker, <i>na,</i> indicating position near the addressee, and <i>ra,</i> indicating distant position. (Dougherty, 1983, p 27)
</p><p>These demonstratives can be traced back to <a href="/wiki/Proto-Oceanic_language" title="Proto-Oceanic language">Proto-Oceanic</a> <i>*ne, *ta</i> and <i>*wa</i><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;5&#93;</a></sup> (Crowley, Lynch and Ross, 2013). <a href="/wiki/Proto-Polynesian_language" title="Proto-Polynesian language">Proto-Polynesian</a> has *<i>eni</i> ('this, these'), which has the reflex <i>*teenei</i> ('this') and <i>*(ee)nei</i> ('these') in <a href="/wiki/Proto-Nuclear-Polynesian" class="mw-redirect" title="Proto-Nuclear-Polynesian">Proto-Nucelar-Polynesian</a>. This implies an evolution from <i>*ne</i> in Proto-Oceanic, to <i>*eni</i> in Proto-Polynesian, to *<i>teenei</i> and <i>*(ee)nei</i> in PNP, which then became <i>*nei</i> in Futuna-Aniwa. However, Proto-Polynesian also has the marker <i>*ni,</i> referring to location close to the speaker in either time or space; in Proto-Nuclear-Polynesian, this became *<i>nei.</i> Dougherty (1983) lists both of these as origins of Futuna-Aniwa *<i>nei.</i> Their lexical similarity in meaning complements this understanding.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Articles">Articles</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Articles"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Demonstratives in Futuna-Aniwa are closely connected to the <a href="/wiki/Article_(grammar)" title="Article (grammar)">article</a> system, as noted above in the <i>Pronouns</i> section. Most uses of the demonstratives in the language follow an article, typically used as optional clarifiers for the articles. While articles in Futuna-Aniwa typically go before the noun they modify, articles can also occur after that noun so they occur before a demonstrative. In this way, Futuna-Aniwa's demonstratives follow the nouns they modify, as is typical of most <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a>, particularly in the Vanuatu region, according to the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://wals.info/">World Atlas of Language Structures</a>. 
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">ex:</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">a</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="article" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ART</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="plural number" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PL</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">fare</p><p style="margin: 0px;">house</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">okai</p><p style="margin: 0px;">whose</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">e</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="article" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ART</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="plural number" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PL</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">na?</p><p style="margin: 0px;">these</p></div><p style="display: none;">a fare okai e na?</p><p style="display: none;">ART.PL house whose ART.PL these</p><p style="clear: left;">'Whose houses are these?'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>As the specific articles of the language distinguish by number, we can pair these articles and demonstratives together to give us the following table, as noted in the <i>Pronouns</i> section:
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Demonstrative Pronouns
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Position
</th>
<th>Singular
</th>
<th>Dual
</th>
<th>Trial
</th>
<th>Plural
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>Near Speaker
</th>
<td>te nei
</td>
<td>ru nei
</td>
<td>taka nei
</td>
<td>e nei, a ganei
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Near Addressee
</th>
<td>te na
</td>
<td>ru na
</td>
<td>taka na
</td>
<td>e na, a gana
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Distant
</th>
<td>te ra
</td>
<td>ru ra
</td>
<td>taka ra
</td>
<td>e ra, a gara
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>In the above table, the sounds written correspond to the phonology of Futuna. In Aniwa, the singular would be <i>ce nei, ce na,</i> and <i>ce ra</i> respectively. The singular article in the above table could also be replaced with the singular diminutive <i>ji</i> article, changing in meaning from <i>'this thing'</i> to <i>'this little thing</i>'. (Dougherty, 1983, p 28)
</p><p>The article <i>te</i> here is one of the three forms of the definite article. Typically, the definite article is <i>ta,</i> however <i>ti</i> is used for a restricted set of words, typically words beginning with a stressed vowel <i>a. Te</i> is the last form of the article and is typically restricted to a set of words that often, but not always, begins with a vowel. When combined with the article <i>te,</i> this vowel can sometimes be deleted. (Dougherty, 1983, p 22)7
</p><p><i>Te</i> can, however, be used as a demonstrative on its own. It is the only article with which this can occur. In this case, <i>te</i> comes after the noun, which is typical of demonstratives but untypical of articles. It’s used in this manner as an emphatic article.
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">ex:</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">tou</p><p style="margin: 0px;">your.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="genitive case" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">GEN</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">pua</p><p style="margin: 0px;">back</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">te!</p><p style="margin: 0px;">this</p></div><p style="display: none;">tou pua te!</p><p style="display: none;">your.GEN back this</p><p style="clear: left;">'This is your back!'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>According to Dougherty, <i>pe</i> can also be used in the article position to specify an unmentioned referent. It is often used when making an example, not dissimilar to English <i>like</i> in a phrase <i>like that</i> or <i>like this.</i>
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">ex:</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">tane</p><p style="margin: 0px;">he</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ka</p><p style="margin: 0px;">will.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="future tense" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">FUT</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">penage</p><p style="margin: 0px;">behave</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ana</p><p style="margin: 0px;">just</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">pe</p><p style="margin: 0px;">like</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ra</p><p style="margin: 0px;">that</p></div><p style="display: none;">tane ka penage ana pe ra</p><p style="display: none;">he will.FUT behave just like that</p><p style="clear: left;">'He will behave just like that.'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<p>The three key demonstratives can also be used in adjectival phrases, typically after the noun, although occasionally before; it is in these places that they are not bound to the article, whereas usually they would need an article of some kind. (Dougherty, 1983, p 29)
</p>
<div class="interlinear" style="margin-left:4em"><div style="position:absolute;left:1em">ex:</div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">a</p><p style="margin: 0px;"><abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="article" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">ART</abbr>.<abbr class="gloss-abbr" title="plural number" style="font-variant: small-caps; font-variant-numeric: oldstyle-nums; text-transform: lowercase;">PL</abbr></p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">toro</p><p style="margin: 0px;">sugarcane</p></div><div style="float: left; margin-bottom: 0.3em;margin-right: 1em;"><p style="margin: 0px;font-style: italic;">ne</p><p style="margin: 0px;">this</p></div><p style="display: none;">a toro ne</p><p style="display: none;">ART.PL sugarcane this</p><p style="clear: left;">'this sugarcane'</p><div style="clear: left; display: block;"></div>
</div>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Locative_constructions">Locative constructions</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Futuna-Aniwa_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Locative constructions"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Demonstratives in Futuna-Aniwa can occur in locative constructions. The constructions use either <i>i,</i> denoting static position, or <i>ki,</i> denoting a motion towards the indicated position, followed by a person marker- either <i>ku</i> first person, or <i>ko</i> non-first person, followed by the relevant demonstrative. In first-person, the demonstrative can be omitted. For example, <i>ikunei</i> indicates a static position, with the first-person marker and the demonstrative indicating near the speaker, meaning ‘here, in this position’; <i>kikora</i> indicates movement (rather than a static position), the non-first person marker, and a distant position, meaning ‘towards there (that distant location)’. In designating position near the speaker, the final element <i>nei</i> is optional. The full table can be found below. (Dougherty, 1983, p. 31)
</p>
<table class="wikitable">
<caption>Demonstratives with <i>i</i> and <i>ki</i>
</caption>
<tbody><tr>
<th>Position
</th>
<th>Static
</th>
<th>Moving ('toward')
</th></tr>
<tr>
<th>Near Speaker
</th>
<td>ikunei, iku
</td>
<td>kikunei, kiku
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Near Addressee
</th>
<td>ikona
</td>
<td>kikona
</td></tr>
<tr>
<th>Distant
</th>
<td>ikora
</td>
<td>kikora
</td></tr></tbody></table>
